011                                               A BSTRACT
012
013            SimCLR is one of the most popular contrastive learning methods for vision tasks.
014            It pre-trains deep neural networks based on a large amount of unlabeled data by
015            teaching the model to distinguish between positive and negative pairs of aug-
016
               mented images. It is believed that SimCLR can pre-train a deep neural network to
               learn efficient representations that can lead to a better performance of future super-
017
               vised fine-tuning. Despite its effectiveness, our theoretical understanding of the
018
               underlying mechanisms of SimCLR is still limited. In this paper, we theoretically
019            introduce a case study of the SimCLR method. Specifically, we consider training
020            a two-layer convolutional neural network (CNN) to learn a toy image data model.
021            We show that, under certain conditions on the number of labeled data, SimCLR
022            pre-training combined with supervised fine-tuning achieves almost optimal test
023            loss. Notably, the label complexity for SimCLR pre-training is far less demanding
024            compared to direct training on supervised data. Our analysis sheds light on the
025            benefits of SimCLR in learning with fewer labels.
026
027
028   1   I NTRODUCTION
029
030   In recent years, self-supervised learning has emerged as a promising machine learning paradigm,
031   offering a way to learn meaningful representations from vast amounts of unlabeled data. Self-
032   supervised learning is of vital importance because the success of supervised learning is dependent
033
      on the accessibility of a large number of carefully labeled data, while the high-quality labeled data
      is expensive and time-consuming to obtain. Self-supervised learning leverages a large amount of
034
      unlabeled data to pre-train the representations for the following supervised fine-tuning learning task
035
      without requiring more labeled data.
036
037   Major categories of self-supervised learning methods include contrastive learning (Oord et al., 2018;
038   Chen et al., 2020; He et al., 2020) and generative self-supervised learning (Kingma & Welling,
039
      2013; Goodfellow et al., 2014). Among the various self-supervised learning methods, SimCLR
      (Chen et al., 2020) algorithm has gained significant attention due to its simplicity and remarkable
040
      performance for vision tasks. SimCLR leverages the idea of contrastive learning, where representa-
041
      tions are learned by maximizing agreement between differently augmented views of the same image
042   while minimizing agreement between views of different images. Compared with purely supervised
043   learning, this approach has demonstrated exceptional capabilities in capturing high-level semantic
044   information and achieving state-of-the-art results on various downstream tasks.
045
      While such a contrastive learning method has demonstrated great success from the empirical per-
046
      spective, it remains relatively unclear how the pre-training scheme helps improve the performance
047
      of the fine-tuning. Some recent papers have been devoted to the theoretical understanding of con-
048   trastive learning (Saunshi et al., 2019; Tsai et al., 2020; Wen & Li, 2021). Saunshi et al. (2019) in-
049   troduced a theoretical framework that contains latent classes and presented the generalization bound
050   to demonstrate provable good performance and reduced sample complexity of downstream tasks,
051   but this framework fails to explain the case of over-parameterization. Tsai et al. (2020) provided an
052   information-theoretical framework based on mutual information to explain the good performance
053   of self-supervised learning. However, the aforementioned papers focus on the setting where the
      hypothesis class has limited complexity, and cannot handle the setting where the number of model


                                                         1
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


054
      parameters is larger than the sample size, which is more common in modern deep learning, espe-
055   cially for vision tasks. Wen & Li (2021) considered the over-parameterized setting and analyzed the
056   feature learning process of contrastive learning and the dependence of learned features on data aug-
057   mentations. However, Wen & Li (2021) only analyzed a very specific type of learning task solved
058   by a slightly non-standard optimization algorithm. Therefore, current theoretical understanding of
059   contrastive learning is still quite limited.
060
      In this paper, how SimCLR pre-training method makes improvements in the fine-tuning training
061   of a two-layer convolutional neural network (CNN) is studied. The case we focus on is a binary
062   classification problem on a toy image data model, which has been studied in a series of recent works
063   (Cao et al., 2022; Jelassi & Li, 2022; Kou et al., 2023b). Under certain conditions related to the
064   number of labeled and unlabeled data and the signal-to-noise ratio (SNR), we study SimCLR-based
065   pre-training followed by supervised fine-tuning, and establish convergence as well as generalization
066   guarantees of the obtained two-layer CNN.
067   The contributions of this paper are summarized as follows.
068
069   вҖў We consider using CNNs given by SimCLR pre-training and supervised fine-tuning to learn a
070     certain type of signal-noise data studied in recent works. Under certain conditions on the amount
071     of unlabeled data and labeled data, we establish training loss convergence guarantees as well as
072     generalization guarantees for two-layer CNNs trained by SimCLR pre-training and supervised
073     fine-tuning. Specifically, our results demonstrate that, although the training losses in the pre-
074     training and fine-tuning are both highly non-convex, the training of the CNN will successfully
075     minimize the training loss. Moreover, although we consider an over-parameterized setting where
076
        the CNN overfits the training data, our results demonstrate that the CNN will achieve a small test
        loss.
077
078   вҖў The learning task we investigate is a standard toy data model that has been studied by many
079     recent works (Cao et al., 2022; Jelassi & Li, 2022; Kou et al., 2023b). This enables an easy
080     comparison between the theoretical guarantees of learning with SimCLR pre-training and those
081     without SimCLR pre-training. In particular, Cao et al. (2022) showed that, direct supervised
082     learning on the data model can achieve small test loss if and (almost) only if the condition n В·
083     SNRq = в„Ұ(1)
                  e      1
                           holds, where SNR is a notion of the signal-to-noise ratio, n is the labeled sample
084     size, and q is a constant related to the activation function. In comparison, the label complexity for
085
        SimCLR pre-training followed by supervised fine-tuning is far less demanding: our results show
086     that when the unlabeled sample size n0 and labeled sample size n satisfy n0 В· SNR2 = в„Ұ(1),       e
087     n = в„Ұ(1),
               e     the obtained CNN can achieve small training and test losses. Clearly, our analysis
088     demonstrates the advantage of SimCLR in reducing label complexity in learning tasks with low
089
        signal-to-noise ratio. Our result serves as a concrete example where SimCLR-based pre-training
        is provably helpful.
090
091   вҖў In our theoretical analysis, we introduce many novel analysis tools that enable the study of the
092     SimCLR algorithm. In particular, we establish a key result that, up to sufficiently many iterations,
093     the SimCLR pre-training updates can be characterized by the power method based on a matrix
094     defined by the pre-training data and their augmentations. Notably, although our analysis focuses
095     on a very specific toy data model, we believe similar results on the connection between SimCLR
096     and power method should hold for more general settings. Therefore, this result may be of inde-
097
        pendent interest. Moreover, all of our analysis of the SimCLR algorithm should also hold for the
        case where the data inputs are generated from Gaussian mixtures. Therefore, a side product of our
098
        analysis is the effectiveness guarantee of using SimCLR to learn Gaussian mixtures.
099
100

108
      2     R ELATED W ORK
109
110
      Self-supervised Learning. Self-supervised learning has won great success in application and
111
      has covered many important fields of machine learning, for example, natural language processing
112   (Mikolov et al., 2013; Devlin et al., 2018), and computer vision (Chen et al., 2020). As one of the
113   important methods for vision tasks, contrastive learning began with learning the latent variable of
114   the data (Carreira-Perpinan & Hinton, 2005). Cole et al. (2022) investigated the factors that improve
115   the performance of contrastive learning, but the analysis was still from an empirical aspect. From a
116   theoretical perspective, some works have also been done to understand contrastive learning. Wang
117   & Isola (2020) identified alignment and uniformity as two important properties concerned with
118   contrastive loss, and proved that contrastive loss optimizes these properties asymptotically. Infor-
119   mation theory was also introduced to establish theoretical framework that explains why contrastive
120
      learning works (Tsai et al., 2020; Tian et al., 2020a). Shwartz Ziv & LeCun (2024) examined differ-
      ent self-supervised learning methods from the information-theoretic aspect and proposed a unified
121
      framework that includes them as information-theoretic learning problems. Tian et al. (2020b) pro-
122
      posed a framework for the theoretical understanding of SimCLR self-supervised learning method
123   and demonstrated that the updates of SimCLR capture variations across data points. HaoChen et al.
124   (2021) considered a spectral contrastive loss and performed spectral clustering on the population
125   augmentation graph, but the applicability of this spectral contrastive loss is limited. Tan et al. (2024)
126   extended the idea of HaoChen et al. (2021) to general loss functions by showing the equivalence
127   between InfoNCE loss and spectral clustering. Furthermore, HaoChen et al. (2021) also extended
128   this to more general settings, including multi-modal scenarios. However, the aforementioned pa-
129   pers focused on the analysis of contrastive learning and do not analyze how contrastive learning
130   influences the performance of the following fine-tuning stage. Bansal et al. (2021) presented a new
131
      upper bound of the generalization gap of classifiers by performing self-supervised training to learn
      representations, followed by fitting a simple classifier such as linear classifier to the labels.
132
133
134   Feature Learning Theory of Neural Networks. There are a series of works that provide theo-
135
      retical foundations for feature learning theory of neural networks. Frei et al. (2022) considered the
      benign overfitting phenomenon in two-layer neural networks with smoothed leaky ReLU activations
136
      when both model and learning dynamics are nonlinear. Cao et al. (2022) analyzed the benign overfit-
137
      ting that appeared in the supervised learning of two-layer convolutional neural networks, and showed
138   arbitrary small training and test loss can be achieved under certain conditions on SNR, but the anal-
139   ysis was based on specified initialization distribution. Without requiring the smoothness of the acti-
140   vation function, Kou et al. (2023b) focused on benign overfitting of two-layer ReLU convolutional
141   neural networks with label-flipping noise. They showed that, under mild conditions, the neural net-
142   works can achieve near-zero training loss and Bayes optimal test risk. Xu et al. (2023) demonstrated
143   that benign overfitting and grokking provably appeared in the feature learning of two-layer ReLU
144   neural networks trained by gradient descent on non-linearly separable data distribution. Meng et al.
145   (2024) analyzed one category of XOR-type classification tasks with label-flipping noises, show-
146
      ing two-layer ReLU convolutional neural networks can achieve near Bayes-optimal accuracy. Kou
      et al. (2023a) investigated a semi-supervised learning method that combines pre-training with linear
147
      probing for two-layer neural networks, and found the semi-supervised approach achieves nearly zero
148
      test loss. However, how self-supervised learning improves the training of neural networks remains
149   largely unexplored.
150
151
152   3     P ROBLEM S ETTING
153
154   This section presents the problem setup in this paper. We first introduce the data model considered in
155   this paper, and then introduce the detailed setup for SimCLR pre-training and supervised fine-tuning
156   respectively.
157
158   3.1   A DATA MODEL FOR THE CASE STUDY
159
160   In this paper, we consider a simple binary classification task. We consider a toy data model that has
161   been studied in a series of recent works (Cao et al., 2022; Jelassi & Li, 2022; Kou et al., 2023b).
      This paper is motivated by Cao et al. (2022), which analyzed the performance of direct supervised


                                                         3
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


162
      learning. To enable a direct comparison between SimCLR pre-training followed by fine-tuning and
163   direct supervised learning, this paper adopts the same toy data model as Cao et al. (2022).
164
      Definition 3.1. Let Вө вҲҲ Rd be a fixed vector. Each data point (x, y) is given in the format of
165
      x = [x(1)вҠӨ , x(2)вҠӨ ]вҠӨ вҲҲ R2d . Assume the data is generated from the following distribution D:

172      signal patch, and the other patch is assigned as Оҫ which represents the noise patch.
173
      As is commented in Cao et al. (2022), the data distribution in Definition 3.1 is motivated by image
174
      data, where the data input consists of multiple patches, and only some of the patches are directly re-
175   lated to its corresponding label. Therefore, this data model is particularly suitable to study SimCLR,
176   which is originally proposed for vision tasks. The data input consists of several patches, among
177   which some are signal patches and the rest are noise patches. Following the   вҲҡ notation given in Cao
                                                                                                         вҲҡ

179   when dimension d is large.

      4   M AIN RESULT

      In this section, we present the main learning guarantee of the two-layer CNN given by SimCLR
273   pre-training and supervised fine-tuning. We first introduce several assumptions on the number of
274   unlabeled training samples n0 , the number of labeled data samples n, the dimension d, the number
275   of convolutional filters m, Gaussian initialization scale Пғ0 , and the learning rate О·.
276
      Condition 4.1. Suppose that the following conditions hold for the pre-training and fine-tuning stage,
277
278                                                                          вҲ’2
      1. The number of unlabeled training samples n0 satisfies n0 = в„Ұ(max{SNR
                                                                    e           , 1}).

280   2. The number of labeled training samples n satisfies n = в„Ұ(1).

      While Condition 4.1 gives a long list of conditions, we remark that most of these assumptions are
      easy to satisfy. In fact, the first two conditions in Condition 4.1 are the key conditions in this
292
      paper. The condition on d is essentially assuming that the learning happens in a sufficiently over-
293
      parameterized setting, which is common in a series of recent works (Chatterji & Long, 2020; Cao
294   et al., 2021; 2022; Jelassi & Li, 2022; Kou et al., 2023b). The condition on the number of convo-
295   lutional filters m is mild as we only require m = в„Ұ(1).
                                                           e     Finally, the conditions on the initialization
296   scale Пғ0 and the learning rate О· essentially just assumes that the optimization is appropriately set up,
297   and can be achieved by simply implementing a small enough initialization scale and a small enough
298   learning rate.
299
      The following Theorem 4.2 summarizes the main result of this paper.
300
301   Theorem 4.2. Under Condition 4.1, for any Пө > 0, if n0 В· SNR2 = в„Ұ(1), e     then within TSimCLR =

      Theorem 4.3 above gives an upper bound on the test loss under the condition nВ·SNRq = в„Ұ(1),  e       while

334
      в„Ұ(1).
      e      Note that we have the condition that the supervised sample size n = в„Ұ(1)e    in Cao et al. (2022,
335
      Condition 4.2). Therefore, Theorem 4.3 demonstrates that for direct supervised learning to achieve
336                                                                                              вҲ’q
      small test loss, it is necessary to have a labeled sample size at least n = в„Ұ(max{SNR
                                                                                  e                 , 1}). This
337
      result also indicates that the learning task is relatively challenging when SNR вүӘ 1, as smaller SNR
338
      requires more labeled training data. Notably, Theorem 4.3 also shows that when nвҲ’1 В· SNRвҲ’q =
339
      в„Ұ(1),

341   In comparison, Theorem 4.2 demonstrates that for SimCLR pre-training combined with su-
342   pervised fine-tuning, as long as the unlabeled sample size n0 is sufficiently large (n0 =
                    вҲ’2
343   в„Ұ(max{SNR
      e                , 1})), n = в„Ұ(1)
                                     e     labeled data suffice to lead to small test loss. As previously
344   mentioned, direct supervised learning requires that the number of labeled training data satisfies
                          вҲ’q
345   n = в„Ұ(max{SNR
            e                , 1}). Comparing these results, we can conclude that direct supervised learn-
346   ing requires more label complexity to achieve small test loss, especially in challenging tasks with
347
      low signal-to-noise ratio. The clear difference between Theorem 4.2 and Theorem 4.3 demonstrates
      the effectiveness of SimCLR pre-training.
348
349   Remark 4.4. To demonstrate the practical value of the theoretical results in this paper, experiments
350   on both synthetic and real-world datasets are provided in Appendix A. Our theoretical results on
351   the advantage of SimCLR pre-training and the results of direct supervised learning in Cao et al.
352
      (2022) together indicate that when the signal-to-noise ratio is low, SimCLR pre-training followed by
      supervised fine-tuning may require far less labeled data compared with direct supervised learning.
353
      The experiments in this paper present typical cases where SimCLR pre-training followed by super-
354
      vised fine-tuning achieves a significantly smaller test loss, while direct supervised learning achieves
355   a larger test loss under same label complexity.

360   In this section, we discuss the key proof steps of Theorem 4.2. Our analysis heavily focuses on the
361   pre-training of the linear CNN with SimCLR. Therefore, for the simplicity of the notation, we omit
362
      the superscripts of the data used for pre-training: we denote by y1 , . . . , yn0 the (unseen) labels of
363   the pre-training data, by Оҫ1 , . . . , Оҫn0 the noise patches in the data inputs, and denote by Оҫe1 , . . . , Оҫen0
364   the noise patches in the augmented data inputs. On the other hand, the noise patches in the labeled
365   data inputs are denoted as Оҫ1fine-tuning , . . . , Оҫnfine-tuning . We also introduce the following notations:
366
                                   zi = y i В· Вө + Оҫ i ,   zi = yi В· Вө + Оҫei ,
                                                          e                     i вҲҲ [n0 ].
367
368   The above notation is motivated by the observation that the linear CNN we consider in the pre-
369   training stage is essentially a function of the summation of the two patches of the data input. Further
370                    zi , i вҲҲ [n0 ] defined above are essentially Gaussian mixture data, and hence our proof
      notice that zi , e
371   is essentially based on an analysis of the performance of SimCLR in learning Gaussian mixtures.
372   A characterization of SimCLR pre-training by power method. Our proof for SimCLR is based
373   on a key observation that the SimCLR updates of each CNN filter is very similar to those of a power
374   method based on a matrix defined by the data. Specifically, we have the following lemma.

395   Lemma 5.1 gives an accurate characterization on how the CNN filters are updated during the Sim-
396
      CLR pre-training. In particular, when the initialization scale Пғ0 is small, Lemma 5.1 implies that
                                                                                     (t+1)           (t)
397   each convolutional filter is approximately updated according to the formula wr       = (I + A)wr ,
398
      which is essentially a power method in learning the leading eigenvector of the matrix (I+A), which
      is also the leading eigenvector of the matrix A.
399
400   Spectral analysis of the matrix A. According to Lemma 5.1, SimCLR may approximately align
401   the CNN filters along the leading direction of the matrix A, and the convergence rate depends on
402   the eigenvalue gap between the largest eigenvalue and the second largest eigenvalue. Motivated by
403   this, we give the following lemma on the spectral decomposition of A.
404   Lemma 5.2. Let A be the matrix defined in Lemma 5.1, and let О»i , vi , i вҲҲ [d] be the eigenvalues

465   F+1 , and we denote M вҠҶ [2m] the collection of these filters with |M| = m. In addition, the rest
                 (T      )
466   m filters wr SimCLR , r вҲҲ [2m] вҲ© Mc are randomly assigned to the initialization of filters in FвҲ’1 .
467   Based on this random assignment procedure, we directly have the following lemma.
468   Lemma 5.4. For any index sets I + , I вҲ’ вҠҶ [2m] with |I + | = |I вҲ’ | = 2m/5, with probability at
469   least 1 вҲ’ 2вҲ’(2m/5вҲ’1) , there exist r+ вҲҲ I + and rвҲ’ вҲҲ I вҲ’ , such that r+ вҲҲ M and rвҲ’ вҲҲ Mc .

      fine-tuning, and there also exists a filter in I вҲ’ whose second layer parameter is assigned as вҲ’1 for
473
      fine-tuning. With this result, we further give the following main theorem on the learning guarantees

499   Theorem 5.5 starts with an assumption on the properties of the вҖңsignal-noise decompositionsвҖқ of the
500   initial weights for fine-tuning. This analysis is inspired by Cao et al. (2022) where the signal-noise
501   decomposition is proposed. However, compared with Cao et al. (2022) where the analysis focuses on
                                                                                                        (0)
502   training starting from random Gaussian initialization, Theorem 5.5 is in fact more general вҖ“ wj,r вҖҷs
503                                                                                                    (0)
      are not necessarily randomly generated. In fact, by direct calculations, we can verify that if wj,r вҖҷs
504   are all randomly generated from Gaussian distribution, then verifying the conditions in Theorem 5.5
505
      will recover the condition that n В· SNRq = в„Ұ(1)e     in Cao et al. (2022) which guarantees benign
506   overfitting. Therefore, Theorem 5.5 covers the result of benign overfitting in Cao et al. (2022).
507
508   Now it is clear that combining Theorem 5.3, Lemma 5.4 and Theorem 5.5 will immediately lead to
509
      Theorem 4.2. Therefore, our proof is finished.
510
511   6   C ONCLUSION
512
513   In this paper, a case study on the benefits of SimCLR pre-training method for supervised fine-tuning
514   is investigated. Based on a toy image data model for binary classification problems, we theoretically
515   analyze how SimCLR pre-training based on unlabeled data benefits fine-tuning in training two-layer
516   over-parameterized CNNs. Under mild conditions on the amount of labeled and unlabeled data and
517   the signal-to-noise ratio (SNR), the training loss convergence and small test loss are guaranteed,
518
      while direct supervised learning requires more label complexity to achieve small training and test
      losses. Our work demonstrates the provable advantage of SimCLR pre-training in fine-tuning stage,
519
      which reduces label complexity to achieve a small test loss.
520
521   This paper focuses on the benefits of the popular SimCLR pre-training method for fine-tuning train-
522   ing. Apart from the SimCLR method for vision tasks, other contrastive learning (or self-supervised
523   learning) methods could also be investigated. A more general question could be: How does the
524
      pre-training of representations influence the performance of fine-tuning in the over-parameterized
      models? Various fine-tuning training processes could also be analyzed, including single-task super-
525
      vised learning or multi-task learning. Future works could explore the aforementioned directions.
526
527

      Yamini Bansal, Gal Kaplun, and Boaz Barak. For self-supervised learning, rationality implies gen-
530     eralization, provably. In International Conference on Learning Representations, 2021.
531
532   Yuan Cao, Quanquan Gu, and Mikhail Belkin. Risk bounds for over-parameterized maximum mar-
533     gin classification on sub-gaussian mixtures. Advances in Neural Information Processing Systems,

      A    E XPERIMENTS
649
650
      This section presents the synthetic-data experiments and real-world data experiments to back up
651   theoretical results and demonstrate the practical value of our theory.
652
653   Synthetic-data experiments. The synthetic dataset is generated following the data model in Defi-
654
      nition 3.1 with the dimension d = 400, Пғp = 2, вҲҘВөвҲҘ2 = 10. The training of the two-layer CNN
      model follows the two-stage (SimCLR pre-training followed by supervised fine-tuning) training pro-
655
      cedure as depicted in Figure 1. Here, the number of filters is set as m = 40 with ReLU3 activation
656
      function. The number of unlabeled data in the SimCLR pre-training stage is n0 = 250 and the
657
      number of labeled data in the fine-tuning stage is n = 40. The test loss is calculated using 400 test
658   data points.
659
660   In parallel, we also conduct the training of the two-layer CNN model (3.2) through direct-supervised
661
      learning on n = 40 labeled data for comparison, and all the conditions of the labeled dataset are same
      as its SimCLR pre-training counterpart. The results on synthetic data experiments are presented in
662
      Figure 2.
663

673

      Figure 2: Synthetic-data experiments: Under same conditions on label complexity, SimCLR pre-
675   training combined with supervised fine-tuning (n0 = 250, n = 40) achieves much smaller test loss
676   than direct supervised learning (n = 40).
677
678   Real-data experiments on MNIST dataset. Real-world data experiments on MNIST dataset (Le-
679   Cun et al., 1998) are also conducted. Following the data model in Definition 3.1, a binary classifi-
680   cation problem is considered, where the signal Вө is originated from MNIST dataset with the size of
681   28 Г— 28. In the data model, the dimension is set as d = 28 В· 28 = 784, and Пғp = 200. Figure 3
682   presents the signals and dataset in the real-data experiments.
683   We train a two-layer CNN model with the number of filters m = 16, and the activation function is
684   ReLU3 , and follows the two-stage training procedure of the CNN as depicted in the Figure 1. The
685   training losses and the test losses of SimCLR pre-training followed by supervised fine-tuning are
686   compared with its direct supervised learning counterpart. The comparison is made under the same
687   label complexity condition where the number of unlabeled data in the SimCLR pre-training stage is
688   n0 = 200 and the number of labeled data in the fine-tuning stage as well as in the direct supervised
689   learning counterpart is n = 40. The test losses are calculated using 400 test data points. The
690   results are presented in Figure 4. Figure 4 essentially presents an extreme case where SimCLR pre-
691
      training with supervised fine-tuning and direct supervised learning result in significantly different
      performances on noisy MNIST images despite of the same label complexity.
692
693   In the following part, we conduct large-scale experiments on both synthetic-data and real-data
694   (MNIST) datasets. The basic data settings are the same as above experiments.
695   Large-scale synthetic-data experiments. The synthetic dataset is generated following the data
696   model in Definition 3.1 with the dimension d = 400, Пғp = 4, вҲҘВөвҲҘ2 = 10. The training of the two-
697   layer CNN model follows the two-stage (SimCLR pre-training followed by supervised fine-tuning)

699   activation function. The number of unlabeled data in the SimCLR pre-training stage is n0 = 100000
700   and the number of labeled data in the fine-tuning stage is n = 250. The test accuracy is calculated
701   using 400 test data points. In parallel, we also conduct the training of the two-layer CNN model (3.2)
      through direct-supervised learning on n = 40 labeled data for comparison, and all the conditions of

848
      Summary of experiment results. The experiments above present typical cases where SimCLR
      pre-training followed by supervised fine-tuning achieves a much smaller test loss, while direct su-
849
      pervised learning achieves a larger test loss under the same label complexity. Both synthetic and
850
      real-world experiments match our theoretical results and demonstrate that SimCLR pre-training
851   could relax the requirement of label complexity to achieve a small test loss.
852
853   In the following part, we analyze that whether the result that SimCLR pre-training advantages the
854
      supervised fine-tuning theoretically proved in this paper on CNNs also holds for other models by
      empirical experiments.
855
856   Real-data experiments on simple Vision Transformers (ViT). Real-data experiments based on
857   images of digit 0 and 1 in the MNIST dataset (LeCun et al., 1998) are conducted on simple Vision
858   Transformers. Following the data model in Definition 3.1, we consider the case where clean MNIST
859   images are treated as вҖңsignal patchesвҖқ and are hidden among other вҖңnoise patchesвҖқ. Therefore, the
860
      dimension for signal and noise patches is set as d = 28 В· 28 = 784 according to the size of MNIST
      images, and we set the standard deviation of the Gaussian noises to be Пғp = 5. All images from
861
      MNIST dataset are normalized to have same norm вҲҘВөвҲҘ2 = 20.
862
863   The training procedure involves first pre-training on unlabeled data to minimize the SimCLR loss,
      and then the model initialized by SimCLR pre-training is fine-tuned on labeled data to minimize the


                                                      16
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


864
      cross-entropy loss. The model f (X) : RdГ—p вҶ’ Rm is defined as follows:

869   normalized MNIST image, while the other patches are Gaussian noises (corresponding to the noise
870   patch in our data model in Definition 3.1). Softmax(В·) refers to the column-wise Softmax function.
871   The parameter matrices V вҲҲ RmГ—d , K, Q вҲҲ RkГ—d , vector 1p = (1, . . . , 1)вҠӨ вҲҲ RpГ—1 is a constant

892
      demonstrates that the phenomenon that SimCLR pre-training advantages the fine-tuning theoreti-

      other models such as Vision Transformers.

3105
3106   Finally, the following Lemma C.12, which is based on Proposition C.11, is proved.
3107
3108
3109   Proof of Lemma C.12. Firstly, we prove that
3110
                           вҲ’в„“вҖІ yi f (W(t) , xi )) В· вҲҘвҲҮf (W(t) , xi вҲҘ2F = O(max{вҲҘВөвҲҘ22 , Пғp2 d}).

3111                                                                                                    (C.37)
3112
3113   Without loss of generality, we suppose that yi = 1 and xi = [ВөвҠӨ , Оҫi ]. Then we have that
